The notion of the feedback loop really applies to hyper-rural areas of France, but there is a political issue at play. There is constant political pressure to reduce spending associated with the expansive welfare state, and one of the big expenses that is frequently cut is rural administrative services, like small hospitals or post offices. Add to that the fact that country doctors get older and retire without any successors, unprofitable train lines are left in the dust or outright closed by the SNCF and you have adminstrative and medical "deserts" forming. That being said, you also find people who leave cities to live in the countryside, either because they can commute or work from home, which poses other problems because they sometimes clash with local tight-knit communities (who see everyone not from their village as "hors-sein", or outsider, something I have seen myself).

As I live in this empty diagonal, just next to the LozÃ¨re, I can confirm everything you said in your video.  I could add that most of the villages in this empty diagonal have problem of populaion disappearing little by little. In the smallest villages, there are only aged retired people, which means those villages will inevitably vanish in 20 years. Families never settle here because there's no employment at all, no public service, no shops, no church (the church basement exist in every village, but the Church have no means to pay a priest to be there all the time, and there's a spiritual crisis in the Church anyways).  All this creates empty lands with abandonned houses and villages, roads leading nowhere, and when the farmers leave too because they're paid barely 400 bucks a month while they can make 1300 bucks in the city, well, their fields become forests. I call this the "green desert" : the forests are expanding because nobody lives there anymore.  Another phenomenon is that the few moderate cities in this diagonal are literally absorbing all the departments rural population, creating urban hubs growing bigger and bigger by emptying everything else around. It's like creating population islands surrounded by nothing : the roads have many difficulties to absorb such fast population movements and inter-urban flows. This creates huge traffic jams in those rare cities where people arrive every day at an amazing rate. In the contrary, once you go outside those few ciies, the roads are empty and maintaing them costs too much money for villages having less and less population every year.  And as you noticed, there is no real solution to avoid this. Farmer's life is a nightmare of endless non-paid hours with zero holidays, a ton of loans to pay the machines needed, finding an employee is almost impossible. And while an homeless has 530â‚¬ to live every month paid by the region, as a farmer you're barely doing 400â‚¬. Globalization and huge companies in food industry or food supermarkets have plummeted prices for farms production.  When you visit France you can't know all this of course, but we're very far from being a paradise, even in the countryside ; and even if France is still a rather powerful country, is inhabitants are suffering many complex problems. Recently 130 emergencies services just closed completely because of impossibility to continue to work, which means if you're injured and go to he emergencies in an hospital, they will say you to go back home and call the emergencies ambulances who will carry you back to the emergencies if necessary. Such dramatic issues can be see in many state agencies unable to accomplish their mission anymore.

I'd say that this is more a statistical fluke than an actual thing (as in "people actively avoided this area historically").  France is a rather maritime country with lots of population living on the coast (as they do in most countries with long coastlines) and one big capital metro that swallows up a lot of the rest.  So there just isn't that much population left to start with (most non-coastal rural areas in France have the same low population density), and if you then proceed to draw crooked lines around the few remaining population centers you get this seemingly "empty" area.  But frankly, you can do the same for most countries. Spain would be exactly the same if the capital didn't happen to be in the middle of the country.

I am in the state of Georgia in the US and we have a very similar issue.  The bottom and westernmost half of the state is all farms.  The northernmost quarter of the state is mountains. The population centers range on an axis alongside the foothills, splitting the rural population into two.   Atlanta dominates with millions of people in its metropolitan area, but Augusta, Columbus, and Macon are all along the population axis.  Savannah is major coastal city and the southernmost of the major population hubs, connected by railway and interstate to Augusta, Macon, and Atlanta.  Unfortunately, because of the way state politics prioritizes land over people, the politics of the state remains dominated by the interests of those in the less populated areas.  Only in the most recent election were the city folk able to overcome with pure numbers, letting have a voice in the federal government, at least.

Comparing the low population density of a *sub-region* of one country to the population density of *the whole* of other countries (Germany and England) is a case of "apples and oranges", and an example of cherry-picking data that's convenient; those other countries also have low-density sub-regions (e.g. the north of England, and most of the former East Germany despite reunification). The same selective statistics goes for (a) population losses (many other European nations were impacted by the continent's multiple wars over the past two centuries, notably Germany/Prussia/Austria-Hungary, Poland, and Russia where losses were significantly higher over the same period of time); and (b) demographic shifts from rural/agricultural to industrial/urban areas, a global trend that is hardly unique to France...a takeaway buried @8:54 despite leading @0:36 with the misleading setup that "partially because of ['the empty diagonal'] France has been diminishing for centuries and continues to do so".

Wrong. most of Germany and all of Switzerland, Austria, Hungary, and Czechia (or Czech Republic) are far from the sea, which contradicts the "sea coast" model. There are two likely reasons for the latter countries success despite a lack of seacoast.   1. Internal navigation by navigable rivers, such as the Rhine and the Danube. 2. Local autonomy. Until 1918, southern and central Germany contained two dozen independent governments who were merely members of a largely voluntary federation. Bavaria, Baden, and the many Thuringian states could govern as they pleased and had their own armies. Switzerland is little more than a federation to this day. On the other hand, France is a highly centralized state. Local languages are suppressed and public policies dedicate resources to the politically powerful major cities. (The US has precisely the opposite political problem, with less populated states having too much influence over national priorities.)   By the way,, there is no "State of Palestine" as you claim -- the Oslo Accords specifically refer to conditions that must be met before the "State of Palestine" can be declared. The Palestine Authority refuses to adopt those conditions. Other conditions of the Accords, including but not limited to ending the state of war and adherence to international treaties, such as the Genocide Convention, are deliberately flouted. Most governments refuse to recognize this so-called "independent state" until a "final status" agreement is signed. Both sides have stopped negotiating.  Please remove this bit of fiction.

The 1918 flu pandemic also substantially depopulated France, much more so in fact than all the World War I fighting. That pandemic, while horrific nearly worldwide, was especially devastating in France. This is not surprising since the heaviest fighting took place there for four years, with millions of men heavily packed into extremely unsanitary trenches, often in rain, mud, cold, and snow. The 1918 flu pandemic killed far more people than all the battles in World War I combined (up to 100 million flu deaths worldwide by some estimates).   Although called the Spanish flu because it was first reported in neutral Spain (which was not censoring its news of most mention of the flu), it killed far more people in France than in Spain. Both sides had to cancel major military operations in the spring and summer of 1918 because too many men were too ill to fight. That flu didn't become especially deadly in terms of loss of life until the second wave in September - November 1918. But the first wave, from March on was extremely contagious and could shut down entire cities, sickening up to a third of the people at a time. And with millions of men heavily packed together in trenches, many units had well over half their men on sick call for three days to two weeks at a time.    The Germans blamed the flu in no small part for the failure of their desperate spring 1918 offensive because speed was critical to it. Very simply, 100,000 Americans per month were pouring into France to train for the counter-offensive that would win the war before year's end. For sheer loss of life alone, the 1918 flu pandemic was arguably the most significant event of the 20th century. Yet strangely the "big history" industry, centered in colleges and universities worldwide, has almost totally ignored the 1918 pandemic for 100+ years.

This was already the situation during the French Revolution, which the committee has realized. People were speaking patois, literacy, healthcare, infrastructure were nonexistent. The first task was to drain the swamps and build roads, because even traveling into the region was impossible. Then revolutionary educators were sent out to teach the population French, but the situation hardly improved even during World War I. The first thing the army had to do with new recruits was to teach them basic French so they would understand the commands at least. Education was so backward that in the 19th century a typical peasant remedy against childbirth fever was to drink wine mixed with wax from a church candle. But the biggest problem remaining was language. It was nigh impossible to make the people speak and read passable French.

The keys is the cost of transportation of goods.  Shipping is the cheapest, so you get ocean side cities and navigable river cities.  2nd is Rail Road transportation, so where railroads were built is the second tier of cities.  3rd is high speed limited access roads, often built near the railroads.  Against all of these is steep terrain that limit cheap transportation.

In Scotland the vast majority of the population live in a narrow strip called the "Central Belt" which, depending on its' definition, has between 2.4 and 4.2 of Scotland's 5.4 million people. I think if the Greater Central Belt is included (From about Aberdeen down to Ayrshire), then I believe that is the area that has the 4.2 million. The bulk of Scotland consists of the Scottish Highlands and islands (with around 9 people per square kilometere (but with many totally empty areas) and the Southern Uplands, with I believe around 22 people per square kilometer. Many people were cleared off the land in the Highlands during the late 18th and 19th centuries, to make way for sheep farming and shooting estates, but parts of it suffered depopulation even since then. The harshness of the climate and the land not being too fertile have contributed to this. Also to some extent, houses being bought up for holiday homes, though in some areas, there are cottages that have been abandoned during the 20th century, but have not been taken over for such purposes.

France has an area of 544,000km2.  A few years ago I was in living the the Western Australian wheatbelt. It has an area of 154,000km2 and just 75,000 people. In other words the area is about the same size as the diagonal in France and with a population of the smallest region in that area.   Both are agricultural areas. I'm sure you will find the same in every agricultural area in the developed world where commercial farming requires fewer and fewer people each year.  Generally this is a positive. Fewer people involved in agriculture means that food costs a small fraction of our incomes compared with the past where food was the main cost.

This phenomenon is normal because of economic specialization driven by technology and cheap energy. It's much more costly to supply scattered settlements with utilities, transportation, food/groceries, health care, and even internet service. In many countries as city living are being improved with better social life, health care, services that take care of chores, housing affordability and of course easy transportation, rural areas are getting even less attractive. Even though this results in wasting rural resources like housing stock, infrastructure, and historical identity, the economic gain of urbanization is much higher. Yes there will be introverts and religious groups moving to rural areas to get away from others in cities, and remote work plus internet services will make this more attractive, it will only be so in very crowded and expensive countries like California and Germany etc. If one day energy is no longer cheap then there will be massive emigration out of the cold or arid countries or metropoli to the fertile river regions of the world, like France or the Mississippi basin.

The US has an empty Center.  If you consider the Great Lakes region to be the "North Coast", and the Gulf coast being the "South Coast", most of the US population is along the four coasts, leaving the center of the country quite empty.  The only large non-coastal cities in the USA are Dallas, St. Louis, Minneapolis-St. Paul, Atlanta, Pittsburgh, Phoenix, Denver, Salt Lake City and Las Vegas. All other large cities are on or very close to these 4 coasts. And apart from the 8 metropolitan areas I listed, most of the Center is empty, especially the Western half of the Empty Center.
The central and widest part of the Empty Diagonal is a hilly and forested region called the "Massif Central", which is fairly similar to Appalachia in the USA.  At the south western extreme of the Diagonal, you have a swampy forested region called Les Landes, which is truly the least densely populated region of France.  The only part of the Diagonal that truly fits your description as being mainly agricultural is the north-western third.

I personally think there is some overthinking going on when trying to explain why some cities snowball and others don't that leads to overlooking a more mundane initial catalyst. The simple fact is we humans go way beyond merely herd like strength in numbers, it is way beyond that sort of simplistic hang out with 100 people rather than 10 means you are ten times less likely to be the one eaten if a lion attacks. With our species the benefits of joining the larger groups is more exponential than linear, being easily able to learn and use enough vocabulary to communicate pretty much any arbitrary concept let us co-ordinate to such a degree that adding to the pool tends to make existing members more productive too. More minds yields more ideas, sure this includes more bad ideas but the useful ones tend to spread better so especially before the internet became essentially ubiquitous allowing near instantaneous communication to any pretty much human on earth at any time having more people close enough to physically go and talk to them led to an exponential growth in productivity and prosperity. So yeah be bigger early prior to modern communications and it would inevitably tend to snowball exponentially from there (Assuming no external limiting factors eg too far from essential resources etc).

I mean, you can take a giant vertical chunk out of the US between roughly the right edge of California and the midpoint of Texas and call that the Great American Void. The vertical slice including Nevada, Idaho, Utah, New Mexico, and friends is largely arid or dry desert.  The low-density farming of America's Bread Basket naturally means less population density.  The area is far away from coasts and there's not as many major rivers (nothing quite like the Mississippi). Interesting and as always well documented facts... yet, those are demographics facts valid not only in France but literally all over the world...  The industrialization of the agriculture - more commonly known as mechanization - leading to the today's modern mechanized agriculture requiring less and less workers, for those who can afford to invest in the latest equipment, is inevitably leading to the remaining workers in the area, now with no jobs and fewer opportunities, moving away chasing jobs and a paycheck in more populated urban areas...  this side effect can be seen all over the Europe and the US and Canada, and pretty much all over the world...  Ironically enough along with using in excess growth hormones, and herbicides, and pesticides, and chemical fertilizers (and who knows what else) not only the quality and flavor of the agricultural products is lower (the cost of higher quantities) but large areas become less populated with significant ripple effects in the economy of those affected areas...  Sad facts and secondary effects of the modernization of the agriculture...  A similar phenomenon is happening already with the modernization of the industry using robots and automatic machines, and that combined with the outsourcing jobs outside the country for cheaper workforce...so by extension corporate greed is already leading to the same effects more people with less jobs, all struggling for a limited amount of resources, and maybe that's why COVID happened and soon more serious and devastating wars will start...  It's a Domino effect...

Regardless of all this, arenâ€™t such areas a necessity for civilization? The most fertile lands are often set aside for food production, and in the aim of maximizing food production, the fewest possible number of people working those lands is preferred, as it means less space being used for housing.  Edit: this got somewhat big. In the current times, this kind of thing is likely to happen, since we have refrigeration, which allows the transportation of food over long distances. So, grow food where itâ€™s easy, ship it to where itâ€™s needed.  Edit2: This comment has gotten bigger. So yes we would actually want the industries to be spread out to minimize transportation, but I feel this mostly applies to artisans; some industries really benefit from mass production, specially things like medicines or materials processing (turning plant or animal products into cloth & leather, or sand & rocks into metals) or recycling, to name a few. Most relevant is probably the creation of meat based foods benefiting greatly from the mass production of feed for the animals, which can be grown far from where the animals are kept & slaughtered.

"Demographic shift from the country to the cities is a global phenomenon." I disagree. It WAS a phenomenon - however, with pandemic, potential global wars looming, economies threatening to collapse AND the advent of remote working due to the advent of the internet, Zoom conferences, etc - people can work from anywhere.  While cities offer the culture of museums, theatre, restaurants etc.... those attributes of a city have been decimated by the pandemic - and people have come up with alternative ways of entertaining themselves - many of which involve connecting with nature. This makes the idea of owning a little land in the country very appealing. Add low inventory on shelves, GMO's, seed soverignty into the mix, a Farm to Table craze - it's all a perfect storm for populations moving back into the country and simplifying.  Trends are towards minimalism not consumerism.

Funnily enough, the country I live in is famous for its beautiful countryside. For untamed areas of wilderness and vast stretches of rural landscape. Alot of our tourism industry is built on exactly that . . . Thing is, the reason its so empty is because 83% of our population lives in urban areas.   11% live in what's termed 'accessible rural' areas   And just 6% in 'remote rural' areas.  Essentially we have a 'central belt' where maybe a little over half the population lives and then a couple of cities and several significant towns stretching north up our east coast. That's it really. The largest place in our 'highlands' is a city with a population of about 50,000  The second largest settlement in the region barely reaches 10,000 inhabitants.
